        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984"><meta property="og:title" content="Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/acsnano.3c04984.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Hard carbon (HC) has been widely adopted as the anode material for sodium ion batteries (NIBs). However, it is troubled by a low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) due to its porous structure. Herein, a graphitized and ultrathin carbon layer coating on HC is proposed to solve this challenge. The as-prepared porous carbon material coated with an ultrathin carbon layer composite (PCS@V@C) exhibits a cavity structure, which is prepared by using bis(cyclopentadienyl) nickel (CP–Ni) as the carbon source for outer coating, glucose carbon spheres as porous carbon, and introducing a silica layer to facilitate the coating process. When utilized as the anode for NIBs, the material shows an ICE increase from 47.1% to 85.3%, and specific capacity enhancement at 0.1 A g–1 from 155.3 to 216.7 mA h g–1. Moreover, its rate capability and cycling performance are outstanding, demonstrating a capacity of 140.3 mA h g–1 at 10 A g–1, and a retaining capacity of 225.6 mA h g–1 after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g–1 with the Coulombic efficiency of 100% at the second cycle. The excellent electrochemical performance of the PCS@V@C is attributed to the ultrathin carbon layer, which is beneficial for the formation of a stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. Therefore, this study provides a feasible surface modification method for the preparation of anode materials for NIBs with high specific capacity and ICE."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/acsnano.3c04984.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6edc16b75775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6edc16b75775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '47b4e67f-e165-4682-8d51-6db8012f1e5b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6edc16b75775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Engineering Ultrathin Carbon Layer on Porous Hard Carbon Boosts Sodium Storage with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.3c04984", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D19063%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDejian%2BCheng%252C%2BZhenghui%2BLi%252C%2BMinglu%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D19%26contentID%3Dacsnano.3c04984%26title%3DEngineering%2BUltrathin%2BCarbon%2BLayer%2Bon%2BPorous%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BBoosts%2BSodium%2BStorage%2Bwith%2BHigh%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D19075%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2023" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Hard carbon (HC) has been widely adopted as the anode material for sodium ion batteries (NIBs). However, it is troubled by a low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) due to its porous structure. Herein, a graphitized and ultrathin carbon layer coating on HC is proposed to solve this challenge. The as-prepared porous carbon material coated with an ultrathin carbon layer composite (PCS@V@C) exhibits a cavity structure, which is prepared by using bis(cyclopentadienyl) nickel (CP–Ni) as the carbon source for outer coating, glucose carbon spheres as porous carbon, and introducing a silica layer to facilitate the coating process. When utilized as the anode for NIBs, the material shows an ICE increase from 47.1% to 85.3%, and specific capacity enhancement at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> from 155.3 to 216.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, its rate capability and cycling performance are outstanding, demonstrating a capacity of 140.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a retaining capacity of 225.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> with the Coulombic efficiency of 100% at the second cycle. The excellent electrochemical performance of the PCS@V@C is attributed to the ultrathin carbon layer, which is beneficial for the formation of a stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. Therefore, this study provides a feasible surface modification method for the preparation of anode materials for NIBs with high specific capacity and ICE.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D19063%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDejian%2BCheng%252C%2BZhenghui%2BLi%252C%2BMinglu%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D19%26contentID%3Dacsnano.3c04984%26title%3DEngineering%2BUltrathin%2BCarbon%2BLayer%2Bon%2BPorous%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BBoosts%2BSodium%2BStorage%2Bwith%2BHigh%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D19075%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291557&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Coating materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Coating%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Coating materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Surface%20chemistry" class="article__tags__link">Surface chemistry</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrathin+Carbon+Layer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrathin%20carbon%20layer">ultrathin carbon layer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Surface+Modification&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_surface%20modification">surface modification</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Renewable energy (solar, wind, hydrogen, etc.) is widely considered to be the most desirable energy source, and its development and utilization have been the focus of concern. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, most renewable energy sources are constrained by geography and climate, making it difficult for them  to meet the practical applications of electricity. Energy storage by means of energy conversion is an effective way to solve this problem. Metal ion batteries can enable later use and transmission by converting electrical energy to chemical energy that exists inside the batteries. Among metal ion batteries, lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are dominant in the worldwide new energy battery market as a result of their high energy density, long-term cycle lifespan, and portability. However, lithium resources account for only 0.0017 wt % of the earth’s crust and are unevenly distributed, leading to a sustained surge in the price of lithium in recent years. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> In comparison, sodium ion batteries (NIBs), with abundant reserves (2.3 wt % in the earth’s crust), wide distribution of Na, and high safety, are considered to be the optimal candidates to replace LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 7 to 12">(7−12)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Within the vast number of anode materials for NIBs, hard carbon (HC) materials are widely endorsed for their commercialization prospects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> However, there is a general problem with HC, where the high porosity leads to low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> This is mainly due to the pore structure that dramatically expands the specific surface area of the material, which means a larger contact surface between the material and the electrolyte as well as the production of more solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) films. Unfortunately, these side reactions are negative for the lifespan of the battery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Thus, managing to reduce the interfacial reactions between the active material and the electrolyte is a feasible method to yield a high-performance anode material.</div><div class="NLM_p">Surface coating is a promising method to protect electrodes by constructing a new robust interphase that avoids direct contact between electrodes and electrolyte, thus reducing side reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Inorganic coating on HC is a common strategy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> However, the inorganic layers do not provide sodium storage sites, and the energy density of the material is bound to suffer some amount of loss. Soft carbon (SC) materials have a high degree of graphitization, which can effectively block electrolytes and serve to increase ICE. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> Thus, SC is also a sought-after material for cladding HC. The easiest operation is to co-blend HC with SC so that SC can appear as a covering effect on HC surface layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> However, this method cannot form a uniform surface coating, and the superiority in the rate capability of nanomaterials is difficult to retain. Another way is to coat SC on the HC surface by solvent evaporation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> Although it can form a better coating effect, it needs to be treated at a high temperature to successfully act as a barrier to electrolytes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">It is worth noting that the introduction of metal ions to lower the graphitization temperature of SC may be a feasible way. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> Herein, an ultrathin clad carbon layer with a thickness of only 15 nm is successfully prepared on the surface of HC by introducing a silica layer as a sacrificial template. This carbon layer utilizes the catalytic effect of nickel to obtain a more ordered barrier graphite band at a low temperature. The target sample, porous carbon sphere@void@carbon, named  PCS@V@C, is prepared. The design of the special structure enables the PCS@V@C to achieve a high reversible capacity (216.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), long-lasting cycling performance and high ICE. It is found that a thin and uniform SEI film is formed at the PCS@V@C electrode after the initial discharge process, reasonably accounting for the enhancement of ICE there.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In this work, the PCS@V@C is designed and prepared by a two-step coating process, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. First, anhydrous glucose undergoes hydrothermal treatment and high-temperature pyrolysis to obtain the PCS, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> which has an average diameter of 1 μm and a smooth surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,d). Next, the PCS is coated with SiO<sub>2</sub> under  alkaline condition. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b,e, the overall decorating of SiO<sub>2</sub> is uniform with no severe agglomeration, and the thickness of the SiO<sub>2</sub> layer is ≈100 nm. To demonstrate the successful coating of the SiO<sub>2</sub> layer, the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> was calcined under air at 600 °C. The result shows that the decomposition of carbon spheres leads to the generation of hollow SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,b</a>). When performing a coating experiment of SiO<sub>2</sub>, the handling must be very careful. That is, the PCS spheres should be well dispersed under continuous high-speed stirring and TEOS should be slowly added to avoid rapid hydrolysis-condensation. If this process is not well controlled, then the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres agglomerate significantly in the coating step (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a,b</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g–j presents the elemental mapping images of the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>, in which it is observed that the SiO<sub>2</sub> layer is uniformly and completely coated on the carbon surface.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the preparation of PCS@V@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a) PCS, (b) PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>, and (c) PCS@V@C. TEM images of (d) PCS, (e) PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>, and (f) PCS@V@C. EDS mapping images of C/Si, C, Si, and O in (g–j) PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> and (k–n) PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the following, a thin layer of carbon is constructed on the outer surface of the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> through a spontaneous heating-induced casting process. The metal–organic molecule, bis(cyclopentadienyl) nickel (CP–Ni), is employed as the carbon source. During the carbonization process, the CP–Ni is melted and spontaneously cast in the voids of the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres, and then with the weight loss and volume shrinkage of CP–Ni, the resultant carbon adheres tightly to the surface of the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres. This spontaneous process needs no complex synthesis steps but direct blends  CP–Ni with  PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> together. The Ni component was washed with dilute HCl, leading to the formation of the PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C. The carbon layer coating is verified by TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>c,f, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3a,b</a>, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4a</a>) and corresponding elemental mappings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>k–n and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b–d</a>). Interestingly, the carbon layer coating is highly uniform and thin, with a measured thickness of ≈15 nm. Such a thin carbon layer is capable of modifying the electrode/electrolyte interface but does not change the overall Na storage mechanism, which is believed to be suitable for constructing high-performance HC electrodes. It should be noted that the mesospheric SiO<sub>2</sub> is critical for fabricating this core–shell PCS@V@C. When the PCS is directly mixed with CP–Ni, the small-size CP–Ni would impregnate into the nanopores of PCS during carbonization, thus no coated carbon layer could be constructed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a–c</a>). Finally, the target product PCS@V@C is obtained by washing off the intermediate SiO<sub>2</sub> layer using 50% HF. The as-prepared PCS@V@C displays a core–shell structure with visible cavities, and the size of the cavity is ≈105 nm, close to the thickness of the SiO<sub>2</sub> layer (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b,<b>)</b>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the structure of the obtained carbon materials, high-magnification TEM images of the PCS, PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> and PCS@V@C are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a–c</a>, respectively. It is obvious that the PCS shows a typical amorphous HC characteristic, and the carbon layer is short and arranged randomly. The thin carbon layer of the PCS@V@C obtained at a carbonization temperature of only 600 °C has a longer carbon layer in comparison with the PCS. This is because the outer carbon layer has Ni atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), which are good for catalytic graphitization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> In this study, Ni atom not only renders the organic moiety of CP–Ni with carbonizability but also promotes the graphitization process.</div><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structure of the materials was further characterized by XRD. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the PCS and PCS@V@C have two diffraction peaks at 22.7° and 44°, corresponding to the (0 0 2) and (1 0 0) crystal planes of carbon, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> The peak positions of the (0 0 2) diffraction peaks for the two samples are almost identical, indicating very close graphitization degree. This is because the measuring depth of XRD is &gt;1 μm, and the thin outer carbon layer (15 nm) shows negligible effect on the crystal structure. For the PCS@V@C, the interlayer spacing of (0 0 2) plane is calculated to be 0.39 nm, and the crystal size along the <i>c</i> axis (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>), i.e., the thickness of (0 0 2) plane is 1.22 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms, and (d) pore size distributions of PCS and PCS@V@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Compared to XRD, Raman can give more accurate surface structural information owing to its shallow measuring depth of ≈10 nm. In this consideration, Raman spectra of  PCS and PCS@V@C were measured. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, the Raman spectra show four characteristic peaks, labeled as D peak, G peak, 2D peak, and D+D′ peak. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> By further subdivision, the D and G peaks can be deconvoluted into four peaks. They are recognized as T band, D band, D′ band, and G band, in order of their wavenumbers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Among them, the two strong peaks of the D band (disordered band) and G band (graphitic band) are located at 1342 and 1592 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which correspond to the defect/lattice distortion and in-plane vibration of carbon (sp<sup>2</sup> carbon), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Normally, the integrated area ratio of the D band to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is an important parameter used to evaluate the degree of graphitization of carbon materials. Herein, the calculated <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value for the PCS@V@C is 1.77, which is lower than 2.11 for the PCS, which indicates that the PCS@V@C has fewer defects, that is, a higher graphitization degree (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Accordingly, the D+D′ band of the PCS@V@C located at around 2900 cm<sup>–1</sup> is sharper, confirming its more ordered carbon structure. We also calculated the microcrystal size along <i>a</i> axis direction (<i>L</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>) according to the Tuinstra and Koenig equation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The calculated results show that the value of <i>L</i><sub><i>a</i></sub> for the PCS@V@C is 10.86 nm, higher than 9.11 nm of the PCS, i.e., the former has a longer carbon layer. The PCS@V@C with higher graphitization and fewer structural defects is believed to be conducive to the formation of a thin artificial SEI film and improve the ICE.</div><div class="NLM_p">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms were used to demonstrate that the coating treatment was effective for pore structure modification. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d, the PCS shows a typical adsorption–desorption isotherm for microporous materials, while the PCS@V@C displays an obvious hysteresis loop, suggesting the existence of meso- and macropores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The specific surface area of the PCS slightly declines after the coating treatment, from 468 to 414 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, it can be found in the NLDFT pore size distributions that the PCS is dominated by micropores less than 1 nm, while the PCS@V@C has a hierarchically micro-/meso-/macroporous structure. The micropores are mainly concentrated at 1.27 nm, and the meso-/macropores broadly distribute between 2 and 100 nm. According to the <i>t</i>-plot method (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>), the micropore area of the PCS is up to 432 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and the meso-/macropore area is only 36 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. After modification, the micropore area decreases to 145 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, while the meso-/macropore area increases to 269 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. In terms of pore volume, the total pore volumes of  PCS and PCS@V@C are 0.178 and 0.445 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Obviously, the dramatic increase in meso-/macropores along with pore volume is due to the cavity structure generated during the coating process and the holes left by washing away Ni, which are clearly observed in the TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9</a>). In order to characterize the effect of pore changes on the density of the material, the compressed density of the prepared carbon materials was measured at a pressure of 10 MPa. The results show that the compressed density of  PCS is 0.80 g cm<sup>–3</sup> and that of  PCS@V@C is 0.72 g cm<sup>–3</sup>. The PCS@V@C does not show a significant difference on the density, although its pore volume increases substantially compared to that of the PCS.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Pore Structural Parameters of Carbon Materials</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> (m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>S</i><sub>mic</sub> (m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><i>S</i><sub>meso</sub> (m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col"
