valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PCS@V@C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">414</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">145</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">208</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.445</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.066</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.345</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the effect of the coated carbon layer on the electrochemical properties, CV and GCD measurements were performed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b display the initial three-cycle CV curves of the PCS and PCS@V@C, respectively, measured at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. During the first scan, an irreversible broad peak appears at 0.3–1.2 V, which is mainly due to the decomposition of electrolyte and the formation of SEI film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> It is obvious that the irreversible broad peak of  PCS@V@C is smaller than that of  PCS, indicating that less irreversible decomposition of electrolyte occurs on the surface of the PCS@V@C. A pair of redox peaks with reversibility appears at about 0.05 V, corresponding to the intercalation/deintercalation reaction process of sodium ions in the carbon skeleton. In addition, it can be observed that the CV curves of the subsequent two cycles are highly overlapping, and the overlapping degree of the PCS@V@C is better than that of the PCS, which suggests a higher reversibility of the Na storage process for the PCS@V@C.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Initial three-cycle CV curves of (a) PCS and (b) PCS@V@C at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Initial galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) rate capabilities of PCS and PCS@V@C. (e) Capacity values of PCS and PCS@V@C at different rates. (f) Comparison of rate capability between PCS@V@C and reported carbon anodes for NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 39 and 45 to 52">(39,45−52)</a> Cycling behaviors of HC anode materials were at (g) 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (h) 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The initial GCD curves at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. Both  curves embody long slope-type curves, with no clear plateau capacity in the low voltage region. The main difference is that the PCS@V@C has a higher ICE and a larger reversible capacity than the PCS. Specifically, the PCS@V@C has a first discharge capacity of 276.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, a first charge capacity of 216.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and an ICE of 85.3%. The PCS has corresponding values of 329.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 155.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and 47.1% in that order. It can be clearly found that the ICE of the PCS modified by carbon layer achieves a huge improvement (≈ 38%), which indicates that suitable electrode surface design can effectively solve the problem of low ICE for porous carbons. This method is superior to most previously reported ICE values (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S2 and S3</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 20 and 38 to 45">(20,38−45)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability was evaluated by GCD at different rates, from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d,e). The PCS@V@C provides high reversible capacities of 252.4, 234.4, 221.9, 203.1, and 187.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at currents as high as 5 and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacities remain at 162.6 and 140.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In comparison, the reversible capacities of the PCS are 149.9, 137.5, 126.2, 110.4, 96.5, 75.6, and 61.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in that sequence. The rate capability of  PCS@V@C is noticeably superior to that of  PCS by a large margin. The GCD curves at different rates are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a,b</a>, which show no drastic change in shape with increasing the current. The cells revert to be cycled at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after several cycles at the ultrahigh rate of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the capacity of the PCS@V@C can still reach 232.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the PCS@V@C still presents excellent cycling stability during the subsequent cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10c</a>). By comparing their rate capabilities, it can be noted that the coated carbon layer gives the PCS@V@C high ICE and capacity while retaining the excellent rate capability. To the best of our knowledge, the PCS@V@C has one of the best capacity retentions at high rates compared to other carbon-based anodes for NIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 39 and 45 to 52">(39,45−52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the rate capability, cycling stability is also an essential parameter for evaluating battery performance. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g illustrates the cycling performance and the corresponding Coulombic efficiency (CE) of  PCS and PCS@V@C at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The cycling performance of  PCS@V@C is outstanding, with a reversible capacity of 225.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles, which is a bit higher than the capacity at the second cycle (216.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The rise in capacity during cycling may be due to the gradual opening of the internal sodium storage sites in the electrode as cycling proceeds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a> In addition, a surprising finding is that its CE already reaches 100% at the second cycle and remains high during subsequent cycles. In comparison, the cycling performance of the PCS electrode is inferior. Its reversible cycle capacity begins to slowly decline from the 150th cycle, and CE requires up to 8 GCD cycles to reach 100%. As we know, the CE is below 100% because  the side reactions consume sodium ions, which can diminish the cycle life of the battery. For this occurrence, SEM analysis was performed on the electrodes after cycling. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S11 and S12</a>, a lot of bulges appear on the surface of the active material due to the high specific surface area of the active material, which induces a large number of by-products generated by the electrolyte at the interface. Excessive by-product generation will inevitably lead to a reduction in battery life. In order to exclude the influence caused by the cladding layer, the cycling performance of  carbonized CP–Ni (C–Ni) was also investigated. The initial capacity value of the C–Ni is 156.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the capacity value at the 300th cycle is 90.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, presenting a continuous decreasing trend. The carbon coated porous carbon sphere (PCS@C) without the introduction of silica has similar cycling stability to the PCS@V@C at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, except that the capacity value is lower than that of the PCS@V@C by ≈50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. However, compared to  PCS,  PCS@C shows a noticeable improvement in the capacity value. By comparison, it can be found that the carbon layer coating can enhance the capacity of PCS and that the cycling stability is also retained. However, the carbon layer coating alone does not directly enhance the ICE as well as obtain the high capacity of PCS@V@C. This suggests that the introduction of silica is a necessary measure.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the cycling performance at a high rate of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> was also investigated. As demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h, the capacity of  PCS@V@C is 199.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the second cycle and still retains 178.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 500th cycle, with a capacity retention rate of 89.8% (<i>C</i><sub>500th</sub>/<i>C</i><sub>second</sub>). In contrast, the PCS was less stable, with capacity values dropping from 125.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the second cycle to 107.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 500th cycle, with a capacity retention rate of 85.5%. After 1,000 cycles, the capacities are 151.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the PCS@V@C and 97.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the PCS. Cycling at high rates, the number of cycles it takes for CE to reach 100% is generally greater than that at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The CE of the PCS@V@C reaches 100% after 10 GCD cycles and gets stabilized at cycle 13, and during the subsequent cycles, a stable CE value is maintained, indicating that the electrode has a high degree of cycle reversibility. The PCS reaches 100% CE at cycle 20, which is probably caused by the large microporous surface area of the PCS. At high rates, the PCS@C and C–Ni exhibit long cycle performance but small capacities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13</a>). In addition, we performed full-cell measurement using the PCS@V@C and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the anode and cathode, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). The voltage window was from 2.0  to 4.0 V, and the rate was 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The charging and discharging plateaus can be clearly observed, which are 3.55 and 3.25 V, respectively. The full cell is able to deliver a high capacity of 228.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (cycle 2, based on the mass loading of the anode electrode). In addition, the full cell maintains a high capacity of 220.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 15 cycles. The high capacity and good cycling stability indicate that PCS@V@C has great potential as an anode for sodium-ion full cells</div><div class="NLM_p">To elucidate the advantages of the PCS@V@C structure, sodium storage kinetic analysis was performed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b show the CV curves of the PCS and PCS@V@C at scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. According to Dunn’s method, the sodium storage behavior is determined by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><mo>×</mo><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>where <i>i</i> is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the sweep speed, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the adjustable constants.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of (a) PCS and (b) PCS@V@C at different scan rates. Fitting plots of PCS and PCS@V@C for (c) the oxidization peak and (d) the reduction peak. (e) CV curve of PCS@V@C at 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, showing capacitive contribution to total capacity. (f) Capacitive contribution to total capacity at different scan rates for PCS and PCS@V@C. (g) Nyquist plots of PCS and PCS@V@C. (h, i) Linear fits of <i>Z</i>′ versus ω<sup>–0.5</sup> in the low-frequency region for PCS and PCS@V@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">This equation can be translated to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> as follows:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>b</mi><mo>×</mo><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>a</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>If a linear fit is made to log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>, then a slope value, i.e., the constant <i>b</i>, is obtained. Typically, the <i>b</i> value indicates  electrochemical behavior. When the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, it indicates that the electrochemical behavior in the electrode is dominated by diffusion, whereas the <i>b</i> value around 1 represents a fast capacitance control process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c,d, the fitted <i>b</i> values corresponding to the oxidation (Peak O) and reduction (Peak R) peaks of the PCS@V@C are 0.95 and 0.94, respectively, which are higher than 0.9 and 0.87 for the PCS. Both have <i>b</i> close to 1, demonstrating that their Na storage processes are mainly capacitance-controlled. Larger <i>b</i> values suggest that the capacitance-controlled part of  PCS@V@C is higher. In other words, the PCS@V@C has faster electrochemical kinetics, which is the fundamental reason for its outstanding rate capability. The high <i>b</i> values of the PCS@V@C are attributed to the drastic increase of meso-/macropore structure and pore volume. In addition, the capacitive contribution to the total specific capacity of the PCS and PCS@V@C is calculated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>e,f, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S15, and S16</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The proportion of the capacitive contribution of  PCS@V@C is higher than that of  PCS due to the faster reaction kinetics. Impressively, the capacitive contribution of the PCS@V@C is as high as 98.2% at 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e).</div><div class="NLM_p">EIS was further used to study the sodium storage kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). In the Nyquist plots, the intersection of the high-frequency region with the <i>x</i> axis represents ohmic internal resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), while the diameter of the semicircle corresponds to charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). The corresponding equivalent circuit diagrams are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> values of both the PCS and PCS@V@C are relatively small, but the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the PCS@V@C (16.6 Ω) is markedly larger than that of the PCS (4.3 Ω), which may be influenced by the hollow cavity layer generated during the coating process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). The small <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values are attributed to the employed ether-based electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> In addition, sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) can be calculated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>D</mi></mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi>A</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>F</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div>where the value of the gas constant <i>R</i> is 8.314 J mol<sup>–1</sup> K<sup>–1</sup>, <i>T</i> is the temperature (298 K), <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode (0.785 cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred during the electrochemical reaction (here <i>n</i> = 1), and F is the Faraday constant (96,485C mol<sup>–1</sup>). The parameter <i>C</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration in the unit cell of the electrode material (here we use the bulk electrolyte concentration, ≈1.0 × 10<sup>–3</sup> mol cm<sup>–3</sup>). The parameter σ is the Warburg coefficient, which is the slope of <i>Z</i>′ versus ω<sup>–0.5</sup>. The σ values obtained by fitting are 14 for  PCS and 68 for  PCS@V@C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h). Then, the diffusion coefficients of the PCS and PCS@V@C are calculated to be 2.93 × 10<sup>–10</sup> and 1.24 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). However, after discharge to 0.01 V, it is found that the diffusion coefficient of the PCS@V@C exceeds that of the PCS. Therefore, it can be deduced that the low diffusion coefficient of the PCS@V@C is caused by the isolation of the open pore structure by the coating layer.</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of  PCS@V@C was also investigated by <i>ex situ</i> Raman spectroscopy. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18a,b</a>, during the initial discharge of the PCS@V@C, the G band of the Raman spectrum gradually red shifts from 1596 to 1584 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The reason for this G band red shift is the intercalation of sodium ions in the carbon layer, which leads to electron occupancy in the π* antibonding band and elongates the C–C bond length. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Meanwhile, the D band intensity decreases during the sodiation process, which may be caused by the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the surfaces and various defects of the PCS@V@C. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> The positions and intensities of the D and G peaks for the PCS@V@C gradually recover after charging, indicating that the PCS@V@C has excellent structural reversibility. This speculation was further confirmed by <i>ex situ</i> XRD characterizations on the PCS@V@C. To obtain the electrodes for the XRD measurements, the cells were previously discharged to a pre-set cut-off voltage and then disassembled and cleaned with sufficient solvent (DEGDME). As can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18c</a>, all of the samples have a broad diffraction band between 15° and 25°, corresponding to the (0 0 2) plane of the carbon material. However, this (0 0 2) peak does not show obvious peak shift throughout the discharge/charge process, because  the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process is dominated by the capacitive adsorption behavior, which is also reflected by the GCD curves and CV results.</div><div class="NLM_p">To elucidate the correlation between the ICE and the SEI layer, depth-profiling XPS measurements were performed to investigate the composition and structure of the SEI film (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>). The electrodes were obtained by disassembling the cells after the first discharge, washed with DEGDME, and then naturally dried in a glovebox. Without etching, the C 1s spectra of the PCS and PCS@V@C electrodes exhibit four peaks at 284.1, 285.2, 286.9, and 289.6 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,c). The low peak at 284.1 eV is ascribed to sodiated Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>–HC electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a> The intensity of the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>–HC peak shows a significant increase after etching (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b,d). In comparison with the change of the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>–HC peak intensity between  PCS and PCS@V@C, the increase of the peak for  PCS@V@C is greater, which indicates that the SEI layer of the latter is thinner than that of the former. In addition, the C–O and C═O peaks show similar change trends with the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>–HC peak between the PCS and PCS@V@C, indicating that the PCS@V@C has less ROCOONa. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a> Interestingly, the change trends are  opposite for the C═O of C 1s and Na–O/C═O of O 1s, indicating that the SEI film of the PCS has higher contents of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and ROCOONa than that of the PCS@V@C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). The F 1s spectra of the PCS and PCS@V@C show mainly two characteristic peaks, P–F (687.6 eV) and Na–F (684.6 eV), which are probably from the reductive decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> anion solvates. After etching, the NaF content appears to be elevated, especially for the PCS@V@C, indicating that its distribution is less in the surface layer and more in the inner layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The P 2p spectra of  PCS and PCS@V@C contain mainly P═O (137.6 eV) and P–F (133.2 eV), derived from NaPO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub> species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 59 to 61">(59−61)</a> After etching, the P–F peak of the PCS undergoes an obvious increase, while the O–P═O peak experiences a decrease in intensity, and this phenomenon can be deduced that Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub> is mainly distributed in the inner layer of SEI, and the outer layer is mainly NaPO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub>. The thicker SEI layer formed on the PCS is also illustrated once again by the peak signal changes of the O–P═O and P–F peaks. The peak at 1,071.8 eV in the Na 1s XPS further confirms the presence of Na–F (NaF) and Na–O (Na<sub>2</sub>O) bonds for Na-containing inorganic compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XPS and fitting results of (a, b) PCS and (c, d) PCS@V@C before and after Ar etching (5 nm).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the SEI film, EIS measurements were performed on the cells after the first discharge to 0.3 V to study the effect of the SEI layer on the impedance. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, the semicircle corresponds to the SEI layer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref62" aria-label="reference 19 and 62">(19,62)</a> The corresponding equivalent circuit diagram is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17b</a>. As a result of the fitting, the overall resistance of the PCS@V@C is smaller than that of the PCS, which is attributed to the filling of the void region of the PCS@V@C after discharge.The <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> (formed during the first GCD process) values are 9.13  and 6.19 Ω for the PCS and PCS@V@C, respectively, suggesting that the PCS@V@C forms thinner SEI layer than the PCS. The low SEI resistance of the PCS@V@C accounts for its high ICE, excellent rate kinetics, and cycling stability.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/medium/nn3c04984_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Nyquist plots of PCS and PCS@V@C after discharge to 0.3 V. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of (b, c) PCS and (d, e) PCS@V@C after discharge to 0.01 V. Dark-field TEM images and corresponding elemental mapping images of (f, g) PCS and (h, i) PCS@V@C after discharge to 0.01 V. (j) Schematic of the influence of the artificial SEI layer coating on HC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/asset/images/large/nn3c04984_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.3c04984&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To better understand the different electrochemical properties, high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) measurements were performed to investigate the structure and morphology of the SEI layer at the carbon electrode interface. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 7</a>b–e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.3c04984/suppl_file/nn3c04984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S20</a> show the HRTEM images and  corresponding elemental mappings of the carbon electrodes after the initial discharge, respectively. A SEI layer with 25–50 nm heterogeneous thickness is observed on the PCS electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b,c). The composition of the SEI layer contains amorphous and crystalline regions. It is worth noting that the SEI layer has a lot of small dark spots, and the lattice fringes of some components can be obtained by local magnification. Among them, lattice fringes with a <i>d</i> spacing of 0.21 nm might correspond to the (1 1 2) plane of NaF. The <i>d</i> spacings of 0.36  and 0.44 nm might be identified as the (1 1 1) and (2 0 1) crystal planes of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, respectively. The <i>d</i> spacings of 0.19 and 0.31 nm might accord to the (2 2 0) and (1 1 1) crystal planes of Na<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. This is consistent with the XPS results discussed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>. In contrast, a uniform and thin SEI layer is formed on the surface of the PCS@V@C electrode with a thickness between 5 and 15 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d,e). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e shows that there are a few lattice fringes in its SEI layer, and the lattice size is also small. The stable SEI morphology on the PCS@V@C is owing to the surface layer with a graphitized ultrathin carbon layer, which effectively reduces the contact area between electrolyte and active material, and the electrolyte decomposition products can be deposited stably and uniformly at the interface as well. In addition, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f–i displays the dark-field TEM images and the corresponding elemental mapping images after the initial discharge, visualizing the distribution of the elements (C, Na, F, O, and P) in the carbon electrodes. It is observed that the SEI distribution on the PCS surface is uneven and thick, while the SEI film on the PCS@V@C electrode surface is uniformly coated. For the PCS, the distribution of Na and F elements in the SEI film composition is found to be higher from the corresponding elemental images, which is consistent with the elemental distribution results from HRTEM, while the distribution of C, O, and P elements is limited. The homogeneous and stable SEI layer is consistent with the low <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values of the PCS@V@C electrode, which indicates a high ICE and excellent rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The ultrathin carbon layer coating effectively improves the electrochemical performance (ICE, reversible capacity, and cycling stability) of HC for NIBs. The effect of this coating on HC is shown schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>j. Because of different pore structures, the PCS and PCS@V@C have different electrode/electrolyte interfaces. The micropores of the PCS are directly exposed to the electrolyte, therefore, not only the outer surface, but also the micropores of the PCS form SEI film, resulting in a large irreversible capacity loss and low ICE. As for the PCS@V@C, although it also has a specific surface area of &gt;400 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, its micropores are partially filled and the mesopores are dominated, meaning that its effective contact area with the electrolyte is lower than that of the PCS. The Langmuir specific surface area calculated based on monolayer N<sub>2</sub> adsorption model of the PCS@V@C is 414 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, lower than 468 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> of the PCS. In addition, the micropores of the PCS@V@C are encapsulated by the outer carbon layer, and between the micropores and the carbon layer are mesopores. The carbon layer and the mesopores separate the PCS from the electrolyte, so that the micropores are not in direct contact with the electrolyte, and it is difficult to form the SEI film. In other words, the SEI film of  PCS@V@C is mainly formed on its outer surface. Besides, compared to  PCS,  PCS@V@C with the carbon layer coating is less defective, which is favorable for the formation of a more uniform and thinner SEI film. For these reasons, although the BET specific surface areas of the PCS and PCS@V@C are very close, the latter forms fewer SEI films and shows a higher ICE than the former.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, a core–shell PCS@V@C has been prepared and used as the anode material for NIBs. By utilizing a silica layer as a sacrificial template and Ni catalytic effect in the preparation process, an ultrathin carbon coating layer (≈15 nm) has been introduced on porous HC material. The ultrathin carbon layer as an artificial SEI layer effectively isolates the contact between electrolyte and active material, minimizes the irreversible decomposition of electrolyte, and promotes the formation of stable SEI. As a result, the as-prepared PCS@V@C offers a high reversible capacity of 217 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, an ICE of 85.3%, excellent cycling (225.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and rate capability (140.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). This work demonstrates a way to preserve the active material pore structure to enhance ICE, which is believed to be a general strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance of porous electrode materials.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Preparation of Porous Carbon Sphere (PCS)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">PCS was synthesized according to the hydrothermal method previously reported in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> First, 8 g of anhydrous glucose was dissolved in 80 mL of deionized (DI) water, and the solution was hydrothermally treated at 180 °C for 8 h in a poly tetra-fluoro-ethylene (PTFE)-lined stainless-steel autoclave. The suspension was filtered, washed, dried, and the obtained powder was subsequently placed in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere and carbonized at 800 °C for 2 h to obtain PCS.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Preparation of PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as-prepared PCS (0.6 g) was added to a beaker containing 80 mL C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH and 20 mL H<sub>2</sub>O, and the mixture was ultrasonically dispersed for 10 min, then transferred to a three-necked flask and mechanically stirred at 300 rpm for 30 min. Next, 0.3 g of hexadecyl trimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) was added, and the mixture was mechanically stirred for 2 h. Subsequently, 2 mL of NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (25 wt %) was added, followed by slow dropwise addition of tetraethyl orthosilicate (TEOS). After that, the suspension of PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> was obtained by mechanical stirring for 6 h. The PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> was collected after centrifugation, alternately washed  three times with water and ethanol, and dried at 80 °C for 12 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Preparation of PCS@V@C</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as-prepared PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> and bis(cyclopentadienyl) nickel (CP–Ni) powder were homogeneously mixed with grinding for 10 min at a mass ratio of 1:1, and then the mixture was poured into a porcelain boat, which was transferred to a quartz tube. Subsequently, the mixture was carbonized at 600 °C for 2 h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, followed by washing treatment with 10 mL of HCl (37 wt %) to remove Ni. In the following, the obtained PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C was washed with 50% HF by stirring for 12 h to prepare the target three-dimensional carbon sphere (PCS@V@C).</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the samples was observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, SU8010, Hitachi, Japan) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Talos F200S, FEI, Czech). Energy-dispersive spectrum (EDS) analysis images were obtained with a Talos F200S TEM system. The crystal structure of the samples was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, D8 Advance, Bruker, Germany). The porous structure was investigated by a surface area and porosimetry analyzer system (ASAP2460, Micromeritics, USA), and the surface area and pore size distribution were analyzed by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) theory and nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT) model, respectively. The valence states of the elements in the samples were determined by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Escalab 250Xi, Thermo Fisher, USA). Raman spectra were measured using a micro-confocal Raman spectrometer (LabRAM HR Evolution, HORIBA Jobin Yvon, France).</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance was measured using CR2032 coin-type half cells assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O ≤ 0.1 ppm). The working electrode was fabricated by mixing active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride at a weight ratio of 8:1:1. Carbon-coated copper foil was employed as the current collector. Before assembling the cells, the pasted electrode was dried at 60 °C for 12 h under vacuum. Sodium foil and glass fibers (Whatman) were used as the counter electrode and separator, respectively. The electrolyte was a solution of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were collected on an electrochemical workstation (CHI660E, Shanghai Chenhua, China) within a voltage window between 0.01  and 3.0 V. Galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profile and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were measured on a LAND–CT2001A instrument (Wuhan LAND Corporation, China) from 0.01  to 3.0 V at room temperature. Electrochemical impedance spectra were obtained using a CHI660E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua, China) within the frequency range from 0.01 Hz to 10 kHz. For the assembly of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//PCS@V@C full cells, the preparation process of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes were similar to that of the PCS@V@C anodes, in which cathode active materials, acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride were mixed in NMP, and the mixture was cast onto aluminum foil.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at ***. comparisons in ICE of PCS@V@C with literature; comparisons in rate capability and cycling performance of PCS@V@C with literature; EIS fitting results of PCS and PCS@V@C at pristine and discharged states; SEM images of calcined PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> at 600 °C under air atmosphere; SEM images of PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub> prepared by using 5 mL of NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O; TEM images of PCS@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C;                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dunn, Bruce; Kamath, Haresh; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">334</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6058</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">928-935</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lio8d6X5Jc4pg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1007/s11426-021-1016-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges and Perspectives of Covalent Organic Frameworks for Advanced Alkali-Metal Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Science China Chemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>64</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">8</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1267</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1282</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s11426-021-1016-6</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D64%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D1267-1282%26journal%3DScience%2BChina%2BChemistry%26issue%3D8%26author%3DJ.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BXu%26author%3DC.%2BLai%26author%3DT.%2BXia%26author%3DB.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26title%3DChallenges%2Band%2BPerspectives%2Bof%2BCovalent%2BOrganic%2BFrameworks%2Bfor%2BAdvanced%2BAlkali-Metal%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs11426-021-1016-6&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.3c04984&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs11426-021-1016-6&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges and perspectives of covalent organic frameworks for advanced alkali-metal ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Jingyi; Xu, Yifan; Lai, Chenling; Xia, Tingting; Zhang, Beining; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science China: Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">64</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1267-1282</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCCCCS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1869-1870</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science China Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Covalent org. frameworks (COFs) are a class of porous cryst. polymers that have been widely investigated in various fields, including energy storage, photo/electrocatalysis, drug delivery.  The covalent-bond interconnection allows COFs extraordinary chem. and thermal stability, and the porous structure ensures a high ion-diffusion coeff.  These merits compensate for the drawbacks of org. electrodes that are easy to dissolve and have low charge cond., and promote the development of novel electrode materials with excellent performance, environmental friendliness, and low price.  However, the application of COFs also encountered many problems, such as poor electronic cond. due to the large band gap.  Moreover, in some three-dimensional (3D) COFs and stacked two-dimensional (2D) COFs, the huge crystal structure, aligned ultralong channels, and numerous crystal defects usually impede ion transport, and the large mol. wts. of COFs generally decrease the specific capacities.  These issues are urgently needed to be solved.  Here in this review, we summarize the latest progress, core challenges and coping strategies concerning with the use of COFs in alkali-metal ion batteries, discuss the impact of material structure on energy storage, and propose strategies for the construction of high-performance COF-based electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqV8CeEZNlUQbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzBxDk8giV8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhsFGgtrvK&amp;md5=c7cc85c982ad454bb1444ecf64deb990</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/cr020730k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brodd, R.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Before Li Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Winter, Martin; Barnett, Brian; Xu, Kang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This Review covers a sequence of key discoveries and tech. achievements that eventually led to the birth of the lithium-ion battery.  In doing so, it not only sheds light on the history with the advantage of contemporary hindsight but also provides insight and inspiration to aid in the ongoing quest for better batteries of the future.  A detailed retrospective on ingenious designs, accidental discoveries, intentional breakthroughs, and deceiving misconceptions is given: from the discovery of the element lithium to its electrochem. synthesis; from intercalation host material development to the concept of dual-intercalation electrodes; and from the misunderstanding of intercalation behavior into graphite to the comprehension of interphases.  The onerous demands of bringing all crit. components (anode, cathode, electrolyte, solid-electrolyte interphases), each of which possess unique chemistries, into a sophisticated electrochem. device reveal that the challenge of interfacing these originally incongruent components often outweighs the individual merits and limits in their own properties.  These important lessons are likely to remain true for the more aggressive battery chemistries of future generations, ranging from a revisited Li-metal anode, to conversion-reaction type chemistries such as Li/sulfur, Li/oxygen, and metal fluorides, and to bivalent cation intercalations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaMHYSJGIok7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzBxDk8giV8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitlGhsrfM&amp;md5=3a9b1d4d5cd0bc8b0874fd76b8274755</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201300071"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">By using in situ hydrazine monohydrate vapor redn. to form SnO2 nanocrystal/nitrogen-doped reduced graphene oxide (SnO2NC@N-RGO) hybrid material, we have enabled SnO2 nanocrystals to approach the theor. capacity of SnO2 as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries.  The as-obtained hybrid SnO2NC@N-RGO also exhibits excellent properties in terms of cycling performance and rate capability as well as cycle life for lithium-ion batteries, benefiting from the nanosized SnO2 particles, the highly conductive graphene, and the Sn-N bond formed between graphene and SnO2 nanocrystals.  Moreover, this approach, i.e., the desirable confinement of SnO2 nanocrystals in graphene sheets, would be very helpful in boosting the electrochem. performance of other nanomaterials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaeq6er1eKbbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzBxDk8giV8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXislymu70%253D&amp;md5=d2f4520b8658394a82ead01af7868873</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzBxDk8giV8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Cost Orthorhombic Nax[FeTi]O4 (x = 1 and 4/3) Compounds as Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jun; Qiu, Bao; He, Xin; Risthaus, Tim; Liu, Haidong; Stan, Marian Cristian; Schulze, Susanne; Xia, Yonggao; Liu, Zhaoping; Winter, Martin; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Abundant and low-cost sodium, iron, and titanium have great potentials to act as raw materials for large-scale power sources.  Here we report the synthesis of novel orthorhombic Nax[FeTi]O4 (x = 1 and 4/3) anode materials by a solid-state reaction method and their electrochem. behaviors in sodium-ion batteries.  These materials are able to reversibly insert addnl. Na+ ions and show very good cycling stabilities.  In particular, the Na4/3[FeTi]O4 material can deliver a high reversible capacity of 120 mA h/g at 0.1 C, and cyclic voltammetry (CV) investigation proves that there is no phase transformation during testing cycles.  The Na[FeTi]O4 material exhibits an even higher initial charge capacity of 181 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C, and in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results indicate that Na+ ions behave in topotactic insertion and extn. manners inside this material.  Meanwhile, gas evolutions during the initial redox process are analyzed by an operando mass spectrometry technique.  The result suggests that the Na[FeTi]O4 material exhibits an enhanced safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrYpP2zjeftkrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjBgEKPPX2YQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXoslyltL0%253D&amp;md5=08b7fca578775c4acd70c485c25c144c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/eom2.12043"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advances in Materials for All-Climate Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>EcoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">3</span>), <span class="NLM_elocation-id">e12043</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much interest for medium- to large-scale energy storage applications owing to the high abundance and low cost of sodium reserves.  However, a principal concern for the wide deployment of stationary SIBs is their temp. tolerance.  Extreme temps. can deteriorate the performance and safety of SIBs by causing very sluggish Na-transfer kinetics, dendrites formation, and severe interfacial reactions.  The development of all-climate SIBs depends on the fundamental understanding of the chem. reactions at different temps. and advances of all the key components, esp. the electrolyte and the electrode materials.  Herein, we start from briefing the key challenges in developing all-climate SIBs, then examg. the latest achievements in confronting the challenges.  The insights presented in this review are believed to guide and inspire further research interest in designing all-climate SIBs that will hopefully serve as a low-cost, high-performing, and reliable stationary energy storage soln.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzTrHbRJy1OLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjBgEKPPX2YQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhslGitb3L&amp;md5=f5e7165f8e1e0fd6580851e3e64f013c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.11.054"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rice Husk-Derived Hard Carbons as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>127</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">658</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">666</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rice husk-derived hard carbons as high-performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Qiaoqiao; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Liu, Yuhan; Fang, Yuyan; Zhou, Xiaosi; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn ever-increasing attention for scalable elec. energy storage owing to the inexhaustible sources and wide distribution of sodium.  However, to develop feasible anode materials still remains a great challenge for the practical application of SIBs.  Here, the authors report hard carbons derived from a plentiful and deserted biomass of rice husk through a facile acid treatment and subsequent pyrolysis.  The study illustrates that the electrochem. properties of the rice husk-derived hard carbons (RHHCs) are significantly influenced by the pyrolysis temp. because of the discrepancy in their microstructure.  The RHHC pyrolyzed at 1300° (RHHC-1300) shows the highest reversible capacity of 372 mAh/g and good cycling stability and rate performance due to its large interlayer distance and suitable oxygen content.  Also, full sodium-ion batteries are assembled to examine the application prospect using Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C and RHHC-1300 as cathode and anode materials, resp., delivering a high-energy d. of 185 Wh/kg and stable cycling performance.  This work could intensify the fundamental understanding of the sodium storage mechanism in biomass-derived hard carbons.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7xLrD2OlEKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjBgEKPPX2YQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVygsLnI&amp;md5=a6f544f14c93cd2145157d257e916707</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. techniques have been used to study the reversible insertion of sodium into hard-carbon host structures at room temp.  In this paper we compare these results with those for lithium insertion in the same materials and demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases.  Despite the gravimetric capacities being lower for sodium than lithium insertion, we have achieved a reversible sodium capacity of 300 mAh/g, close to that for lithium insertion in graphitic carbon anode materials.  Such materials may therefore be useful as anodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpm125w9mEFSbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljjBgEKPPX2YQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXisVygtL0%253D&amp;md5=7480ac0a7bc2620ce565b1c21f1e2463</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202003640"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Anode Materials: A Detailed Comparison between Na-Ion and K-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Anode Materials: A Detailed Comparison between Na-ion and K-ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Lupeng; Wang, Wei; Lu, Shanfu; Xiang, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2003640</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As novel "post lithium-ion batteries" sodium-ion batteries/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs) are emerging and show bright prospect in large-scale energy storage applications due to abundant Na/K resources.  Further benefits of this technol. include, its low cost, chem. inertness and safety.  Extensive research findings have demonstrated that carbon-based materials are promising candidates for both SIBs and PIBs.  Although the two alkali-ion batteries have similar internal components and electrochem. reaction mechanisms, in carbon-based materials the storage/release behaviors of Na+ and K+ are not exactly the same.  Therefore, a comprehensive comparison of Na+/K+ storage behaviors in carbon anode materials is lacking.  It is absolutely imperative to understand these mechanisms more clearly to achieve ideal performance.  Herein, three potential Na+/K+ storage/release behaviors are discussed, which are (i) intercalation/deintercalation mechanism, (ii) adsorption/desorption mechanism, and (iii) pore-filling mechanism.  This review not only attempts to summarize the development status of carbon anode materials (graphite, graphene, hard carbon and soft carbon), but also provides a comprehensive comparison (mechanism, capacity, rate capability, diffusion coeff., cyclability, potassiation/sodiation potential) between SIBs and PIBs.  Finally, crit. issues and perspectives are discussed to demonstrate possible development directions for carbon anode materials for SIBs and PIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqRvzJnA8ene7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpBq3F85Jk4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXitVSgu7g%253D&amp;md5=3b7edd5107d3db8c8037992f11bc2f2c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1149/2.1361902jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lignin-Derived Nitrogen-Doped Porous Carbon as a High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin-derived nitrogen-doped porous carbon as a high-rate anode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Du, Leilei; Wu, Wei; Luo, Chao; Xu, Dongwei; Guo, Hanyu; Wang, Ruo; Zhang, Tian; Wang, Jun; Deng, Yonghong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">166</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen-doped carbon anode materials have been prepd. by a hard-template (SiO2 nanoparticles) method, in which alkali lignin-derived azo polymer (AL-azo-NO2) was used as a low-cost carbon precursor.  The as-prepd. N-doped carbon materials show a honeycomb-like morphol. with uniform nanopores.  These materials are able to reversibly insert Na+ ions and show promising cycling performance.  In particular, the porous carbon material with larger surface area and pore vol. delivers a higher initial capacity of 205 mAh g-1 in the voltage range of 0.01-2 V at 50 mA g-1, compared to the material with lower surface area and pore vol. (only 155 mAh g-1).  The former also exhibits much better rate capability than the latter.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A g-1, it shows a high specific capacity of 101 mAh g-1 after 1100 cycles, which retains 92% of its initial capacity.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results prove that the enhanced electrochem. performance is attributed to the improved Na+-ions kinetics in this material.  This work presents an alternative approach for the synthesis of high-rate carbonaceous anode materials for sodium ion batteries from low cost and sustainable feedstock.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqrKV1UGXmA7bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpBq3F85Jk4Q"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Anodes: Fundamental Understanding and Commercial Perspectives for Na-Ion Batteries beyond Li-Ion and K-Ion Counterparts</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Ling-Fei; Hu, Zhe; Lai, Wei-Hong; Tao, Ying; Peng, Jian; Miao, Zong-Cheng; Wang, Yun-Xiao; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbon (HC) is recognized as a promising anode material with outstanding electrochem. performance for alkali metal-ion batteries including lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as well as their analogs sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Herein, a comprehensive review of the recent research is presented to interpret the challenges and opportunities for the applications of HC anodes.  The ion storage mechanisms, materials design, and electrolyte optimizations for alkali metal-ion batteries are illustrated in-depth.  HC is particularly promising as an anode material for SIBs.  The solid-electrolyte interphase, initial Coulombic efficiency, safety concerns, and all-climate performances, which are vital for practical applications, are comprehensively discussed.  Furthermore, com. prototypes of SIBs based on HC anodes are extensively elaborated.  The remaining challenges and research perspectives are provided, aiming to shed light on future research and early commercialization of HC-based SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrgUes-Iv_xOrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpBq3F85Jk4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVagtLrM&amp;md5=dc06d1bd2c8724df623e45590cac38e2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.105738"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boost Sodium-Ion Batteries to Commercialization: Strategies to Enhance Initial Coulombic Efficiency of Hard Carbon Anode</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>82</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">105738</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boost sodium-ion batteries to commercialization: Strategies to enhance initial Coulombic efficiency of hard carbon anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Minghao; Li, Yu; Wu, Feng; Bai, Ying; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
